            "content": "<b>с╣Ъtu,</b>┬ж тАШseason,тАЩ is a term repeatedly mentioned from the Rigveda<sup>1</sup> onwards, Three seasons of the year are often alluded to,<sup>2</sup> but the names are not usually specified. In one passage of the Rigveda<sup>3</sup> spring (<i>vasanta</i>), summer (<i>gr─лс╣гma</i>), and autumn (<i>┼Ыarad</i>) are given. The Rigveda knows also the rainy season (<i>pr─Б-vс╣Ыс╣г</i>) and the winter (<i>him─Б, hemanta</i>). A more usual<sup>4</sup> division (not found in the Rigveda) is into five seasons, <i>vasanta, gr─лс╣гma, varс╣г─Б, ┼Ыarad, hemama-si┼Ыira,</i> but occasionally the five are otherwise divided, <i>varс╣г─Б-┼Ыarad</i> being made one season.<sup>5</sup> Sometimes six<sup>6</sup> seasons are reckoned, <i>hemanta</i> and <i>┼Ыi┼Ыira</i> being divided, so that the six seasons can be made parallel to the twelve months of the year. A still more artificial arrangement<sup>7</sup> makes the seasons seven, possibly by reckoning the intercalary month as a season, as Weber and Zimmer<sup>8</sup> hold, or more probably because of the predilection for the number seven, as Roth<sup>9</sup> suggests. Occasionally the word <i>с╣Ыtu</i> is applied to the months.<sup>10</sup> The last season, according to the ┼Ъatapatha Br─Бhmaс╣Зa,<sup>11</sup> is <i>hemanta.</i> <dvsn n='P'/>The growth of the division of the seasons from three to five is rightly explained by Zimmer<sup>12</sup> as indicating the advance of the Vedic Indians towards the east. It is not Rigvedic, but dominates the later Saс╣Гhit─Бs. Traces of an earlier division of the year into winter and summer do not appear clearly in the Rigveda, where the appropriate words <i>him─Б</i> and <i>sam─Б</i> are merely general appellations of the year, and where <i>┼Ыarad</i><sup>13</sup> is commoner than either as a designation of the year, because it denotes the harvest, a time of overwhelming importance to a young agricultural people. The division of the year in one passage of the Atharvaveda<sup>14</sup> into two periods of six months is merely formal, and in no way an indication of old tradition. <F>1) i. 49, 3; 84, 18, etc.</F> <F>2) <i>Cf.</i> Rv. i. 164, 2 (<i>tri-n─Бbhi</i>), 48 (<i>tr─лс╣Зi</i> <i>nabhy─Бni</i>); also perhaps the с╣Ъbhus as the genii of the three seasons and the three dawns. <i>Cf.</i> Macdonell, <i>Vedic</i> <i>Mythology,</i> p. 133; Hillebrandt, <i>Vedische</i> <i>Mythologie,</i> 2, 33 <i>et seq.:</i> ┼Ъatapatha Br─Бhmaс╣Зa, xiv. 1, 1, 28, and the <i>c─Бturm─Бsy─Бni,</i> or four-monthly sacrifices performed at the beginning of the seasons in the ritual (Weber, <i>Naxatra,</i> 2, 329 <i>et seq.</i>).</F> <F>3) x. 90, 6.
